<div id="review_56092431" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56092431" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56092431?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2009-read">2009-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2009-worst10">2009-worst10</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=from-library">from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=british-historical">british-historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56092431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer545352725633214017">Maybe I'm just not cut out for Dan Simmons' particular brand of mysticism. I didn't like the supernatural bent of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3974.The_Terror_A_Novel' title='The Terror A Novel by Dan Simmons' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Terror</a> and didn't like the supernatural bent of this book. What appears to be a suspenseful Dickensian supernatural mystery is actually, beneath the surface, an incredibly long and dull tour of Victorian London and opium dreams.<br/><br/>The jacket copy of this edition misconstrues the book's nature, at least in my opinion. When I borrowed this book, I thought I was getting a supernatural </span>
  <span id="freeText545352725633214017" style="display:none">Maybe I'm just not cut out for Dan Simmons' particular brand of mysticism. I didn't like the supernatural bent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3974.The_Terror_A_Novel" title="The Terror A Novel by Dan Simmons" rel="nofollow noopener">The Terror</a> and didn't like the supernatural bent of this book. What appears to be a suspenseful Dickensian supernatural mystery is actually, beneath the surface, an incredibly long and dull tour of Victorian London and opium dreams.<br /><br />The jacket copy of this edition misconstrues the book's nature, at least in my opinion. When I borrowed this book, I thought I was getting a supernatural mystery told from the point of view of Charles Dickens (or perhaps following him from a limited third-person perspective). Instead, the actual narrator of the book is Dickens' friend and protégé, Wilkie Collins. In fact, I would go as far as saying that the book isn't about Dickens at all. It's more about Wilkie Collins, and Dickens is only involved because the majority of the public will have never heard of Collins but would love, like me, to read a book about Dickens. Now this is all well and good--I didn't mind reading a book about Wilkie Collins. I just wish I had known that going into the book.<br /><br />Similarly, I shouldn't be upset about supernatural elements in a book that is supposed to be supernatural, right? Except that the entire "Drood" mystery is conflated by the prospect of it all being an opium- or mesmerism-induced fantasy. Perhaps I just dislike it when the supernatural elements aren't blatantly real but merely just suggested.<br /><br />Almost everyone mentions the unnecessary length of <i>Drood</i>, and it's a valid point. There's no reason for this novel to be nearly 800 pages. Simmons does a wonderful job describing Victorian London, and I liked being immersed in that world. But he could have ... summarized certain episodes. The first two hundred or so pages are enjoyable, and then the book's supernatural aspect goes into overdrive, and it becomes tiresome.<br /><br />This is just a book worth skipping, period. It doesn't matter how much you love Victorian London, Charles Dickens, or Wilkie Collins. There's no reason to subject yourself to <i>Drood</i>. The payoff isn't worth it. Go read an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6331.Anne_Perry" title="Anne Perry" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne Perry</a> mystery instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="545352725633214017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_45875382" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45875382" />
    <a title="Jeanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1042102-jeanne"><img alt="Jeanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308183903p2/1042102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45875382?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanne" href="/user/show/1042102-jeanne">Jeanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45875382" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6968824634740579255">This is the first book I've read by Dan Simmons but it certainly won't be the last. I was drawn to this particular book because of my love for the works of Charles Dickens, but I knew I had to read it after attending a book signing where Mr. Simmons talked about the book and its "unreliable" narrator, Wilkie Collins. I was not disappointed! <br/><br/>The richness and depth of Mr. Simmons research and prose is exquisite. It is the sort of book one must immerse one's self into. I nearly felt the stays of my</span>
  <span id="freeText6968824634740579255" style="display:none">This is the first book I've read by Dan Simmons but it certainly won't be the last. I was drawn to this particular book because of my love for the works of Charles Dickens, but I knew I had to read it after attending a book signing where Mr. Simmons talked about the book and its "unreliable" narrator, Wilkie Collins. I was not disappointed! <br /><br />The richness and depth of Mr. Simmons research and prose is exquisite. It is the sort of book one must immerse one's self into. I nearly felt the stays of my Victorian corset poking my ribs it is so spectacularly reminiscent of the writing styles of the period. The story is redolent with details sorely missing from more modern twenty-first century works. I particularly like the way it left me with things to think about for days and days after reading the last pages. Each reader gets the ending they want, really. It's brilliant the way Simmons doesn't tell the reader the ending, but lets you imagine your ending. I would venture a guess that if you asked ten different people the ending, you would get then different answers. That, is true artistry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6968824634740579255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_561050188" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/561050188" />
    <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy"><img alt="Tristram Shandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589454223p2/18181298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/561050188?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristram Shandy" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy">Tristram Shandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847249326" href="/book/show/6433279-drood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=contemporary-literature">contemporary-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer561050188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13372330867070451513"><b>Give the Dickens His Due!</b><br/><br/>In the end, Wilkie Collins grudgingly has to do this when, leafing through his late friend Dickens’s <i>Bleak House</i>, he is struck with the superior genius that lies in Dickens’s use of language. “The book was the style and the style was the man. And the man was – had been – Charles Dickens.” With these words not only Collins, but seemingly also Dan Simmons, author of the novel <i>Drood</i>, himself offers homage to the novelist Charles Dickens.<br/><br/>And yet it is a strange tribute that </span>
  <span id="freeText13372330867070451513" style="display:none"><b>Give the Dickens His Due!</b><br /><br />In the end, Wilkie Collins grudgingly has to do this when, leafing through his late friend Dickens’s <i>Bleak House</i>, he is struck with the superior genius that lies in Dickens’s use of language. “The book was the style and the style was the man. And the man was – had been – Charles Dickens.” With these words not only Collins, but seemingly also Dan Simmons, author of the novel <i>Drood</i>, himself offers homage to the novelist Charles Dickens.<br /><br />And yet it is a strange tribute that is paid the great English writer. Wilkie Collins, narrator of the tale <i>Drood</i> and actually a friend and co-writer of the Inimitable’s, is careful to present Dickens in a poor light, enlarging on his harsh treatment of his wife Catherine and his romantic affair with the actress Ellen Ternan and availing himself of any opportunity to draw Dickens as a pompous, vane and self-righteous ageing man, who, ludicrously, even preens himself on his capacities of mesmerism. The tale starts with Dickens’s escape from death in the Staplehurst Rail Disaster on June 9th, 1865 and his vision of an uncanny individual named Drood, who seems, as he walks about among the injured passengers, to take one victim’s life after the other’s. From now on, Dickens is haunted by the idea of Drood and goes out of his way to find him in his hideout in the London sewers. Collins, who is at first rather sceptic about the existence of Drood, by and by finds himself drawn into the machinations of Drood and his underlings on the one hand, and of the dishonourably discharged ex-inspector Field on the other, who is absolutely convinced of having, in Drood, the mastermind of English crime under his very feet, somewhere in the London drains, and who imbues his foe with preternatural powers as he talks about him.<br /><br />What comes over as a flaw of Simmons’s novel is its tendency to lengthiness and procrastination due to ample biographic detail on both Dickens and Collins and their respective families, it being hard to figure out, at the same time, where fact and fiction interweave. This melange, to my mind, would only seem enjoyable to the dyed-in-the-wool Dickens, Collins, Victorian novel aficionado. The general effect on the average reader, however, might be quite a put-off – all the more so as Drood at times seems to dwindle away from the plot, which is suddenly about Collins’s suspecting Dickens of murder and eventually about the narrator’s plotting murder most foul himself. This might not go down well with impatient readers.<br /><br />What I like most about the novel is Wilkie Collins as a highly unreliable narrator. First of all, he is drifting more and more into drug-addiction – although he claims to be taking increasing doses of laudanum and opium for medical reasons. Apart from that his drug-fevered brain is fed by both Dickens and the dodgy ex-inspector Field with gruesome stories about Drood’s powers. Last but not least, Collins’s envy for his superior novelist-friend further addles his perspective on things. Whereas it is quite easy for us readers to notice the hallucinatory character of Collins’s <i>doppelgänger</i> and a mysterious spectre-woman, there are other instances in which we are at a loss about what we can believe and what we had better take with a grain of salt. Although Collins professes his intention to tell us exclusively about Dickens and Drood, he invariably reveals a lot about his own life, details which are hardly to his credit (especially his dire treatment of his mistress Caroline G., while at the same time he animadverts upon Dickens’s ousting his wife). In all this, Simmons’s choice of viewpoint is marvellous, – increasingly so, as he manages to adopt a local and temporal colour style of writing, with only sometimes the contemporary American writer’s style being tangible.<br /><br />Equally intriguing, there are various allusions to Dickens’s and Collins’s novels, such as the dull, yet not to be underestimated stonemason Dradles, who would later make his appearance as Durdles in <i>The Mystery of Edwin Drood</i>, or the writers’ comments on each others’ and their own works. <i>Drood</i> is therefore not only a mystery thriller and a study of a mind twisted by jealousy and drugs, but also a book about writing books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13372330867070451513" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2142978356" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2142978356" />
    <a title="Beverly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68708752-beverly"><img alt="Beverly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505852430p2/68708752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2142978356?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beverly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beverly" href="/user/show/68708752-beverly">Beverly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2142978356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14668906723367883493">At 771 pages, this book about the rivalry between Charles Dickens and his friend Wilkie Collins was way too long. The two men were rivals and friends, in real life; Dickens wrote to a friend that he found The Moonstone unbearable and Collins in a letter does not seem to be overly upset at Dickens' death. This is a brilliant mix of real and fictional bits of their lives. Collins even has an epiphany towards the end that he could never compare to Dickens' greatness, as he's reading Bleak House, Co</span>
  <span id="freeText14668906723367883493" style="display:none">At 771 pages, this book about the rivalry between Charles Dickens and his friend Wilkie Collins was way too long. The two men were rivals and friends, in real life; Dickens wrote to a friend that he found The Moonstone unbearable and Collins in a letter does not seem to be overly upset at Dickens' death. This is a brilliant mix of real and fictional bits of their lives. Collins even has an epiphany towards the end that he could never compare to Dickens' greatness, as he's reading Bleak House, Collins comes across this section,". . . 'when the sun shone through the clouds, making silvery pools in a dark sea.' <br />Silvery pools in the dark sea.<br />Pools in the sea.<br />. . .and I knew in that instant, . . . passing over these few words in these short sentences, that I would never----not ever. . .be able to think and write like that."</span>
  <a data-text-id="14668906723367883493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1857336543" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1857336543" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/997203-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529787483p2/997203.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1857336543?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/997203-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1857336543" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3001217909610754129">I suspect DROOD is the Marmite of novels set in the Victorian era. Like all Simmons' recent work, it is meticulously researched, but there also lies the problem, for he cannot stop himself from showing us that research on the page - not only the bits that are pertinent to the story, but too many of the bits that are merely interesting, but flow-stopping. As in another Simmons exploration of a literary figure, that of Henry James, in THE FIFTH HEART, we get details of dinner parties, lists of fam</span>
  <span id="freeText3001217909610754129" style="display:none">I suspect DROOD is the Marmite of novels set in the Victorian era. Like all Simmons' recent work, it is meticulously researched, but there also lies the problem, for he cannot stop himself from showing us that research on the page - not only the bits that are pertinent to the story, but too many of the bits that are merely interesting, but flow-stopping. As in another Simmons exploration of a literary figure, that of Henry James, in THE FIFTH HEART, we get details of dinner parties, lists of famous literary guests, and explanations of public buildings works that do nothing to further the story.<br /><br />That said, I enjoyed the book enough to give it four stars, for as a writer, it is a fascinating glimpse into how we tell stories, both to ourselves and our audiences, and also how such stories take shape and form - and a certain degree of reality.<br /><br />It also touches on something a lot of writers know but don't talk about - the almost crippling at times green wave of envy and self loathing that comes when one of your friends has wild success. They're still a friend, you still love them, but there's that little voice, deep down, willing to commit murder in the face of their happiness... or maybe that's just me. :-)<br /><br />Simmons' control of point of view and the mechanics of the writing itself are as masterful as ever. The narrator is a fully realized character, although I doubt the real Wilkie Collins was quite so unreliable as portrayed here, for the prodigious quantities of opiates consumed would surely have left a man quite unable to write such great works as The Moonstone and The Woman in White. <br /><br />His friendship with Dickens, and the way it affects Collins and his work, are nicely depicted, and there are some glorious set pieces, in a train crash, in the sewers under London, and in the descriptions of Dickens' live performances.<br /><br />You don't need to have read either Collins' or Dickens' work to appreciate this book, but it does help to have done so, to provide context for a lot of the conversations between the writers, and also the final mystery of Drood himself.<br /><br />I did like it, and it might have got five stars, if it had been two or three hundred pages shorter and tighter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3001217909610754129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4845883387745511182"> So overall, I give this Dan Simmons' Drood a flat four-star rating. I didn't quite love the story itself, so that's the removed fifth star. <br/><br/> But why four stars (if I admitted I didn't care all that much about the story)? All the elements that make this a Dan Simmons novel, for me, is what propelled me forward. The narration and style of storytelling, for one thing. The meticulous research and historical accuracy, for another. The sense of time and place, the setting coming alive, the beautiful </span>
  <span id="freeText4845883387745511182" style="display:none"> So overall, I give this Dan Simmons' Drood a flat four-star rating. I didn't quite love the story itself, so that's the removed fifth star. <br /><br /> But why four stars (if I admitted I didn't care all that much about the story)? All the elements that make this a Dan Simmons novel, for me, is what propelled me forward. The narration and style of storytelling, for one thing. The meticulous research and historical accuracy, for another. The sense of time and place, the setting coming alive, the beautiful old-country scenery, the foul stench of 1800s London, the crowded graveyards overflowing corpses and bones, etc... I'm guessing the book's content is what made me love Drood. I even gained a new appreciation for old literature and wanting to read some original Dickens novels. <br /> <br /> I know that this is a pretty weak review, but this wasn't one of those books that left a lasting impression on me; however I'm very glad I read it, and gained a lot of historical perspective from it.<br /><br /><br /> </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3487774345353053987">I finished this several weeks ago, and it's stuck with me so much that I feel compelled to review even if it will be a brief one. Simmons takes is back to the Victorian age, and he does so with such great detail that I felt that I traveled back in time a bit. The first half is a bit slow, and yet I eagerly returned to it in what free time I had. Simmons is developing the characters until they are full dimensional. The narrator, Wilkie Collins, froths and rages about the injustices cast on him by</span>
  <span id="freeText3487774345353053987" style="display:none">I finished this several weeks ago, and it's stuck with me so much that I feel compelled to review even if it will be a brief one. Simmons takes is back to the Victorian age, and he does so with such great detail that I felt that I traveled back in time a bit. The first half is a bit slow, and yet I eagerly returned to it in what free time I had. Simmons is developing the characters until they are full dimensional. The narrator, Wilkie Collins, froths and rages about the injustices cast on him by Dickens and yet he still follows him. We realize things are not what they appear at all, the narrator is a very suspect and untrustworthy fellow! Once things kick in, the book becomes a rapid page-turner mystery/thriller. I had never heard of Wilkie before (I'm not a Dickens lover), but I got so fascinated I googled him, and am now planning on reading A Woman in White.<br /><br /></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1132543644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer600383836787490083">I have a confession. I have never read any Charles Dickens. I have never wanted to read any Charles Dickens. I have seen several versions of A Christmas Carol. So, I can say that I know nothing of Charles Dickens. I suspect this is why I liked this book. I do not have him on some kind a pedestal only to be disappointed by his being human. I had never heard of Wilkie Collins either. I imagine he does not share that same pedestal as his dear friend Charles. <br/><br/>I can say that this was an amazing blend</span>
  <span id="freeText600383836787490083" style="display:none">I have a confession. I have never read any Charles Dickens. I have never wanted to read any Charles Dickens. I have seen several versions of A Christmas Carol. So, I can say that I know nothing of Charles Dickens. I suspect this is why I liked this book. I do not have him on some kind a pedestal only to be disappointed by his being human. I had never heard of Wilkie Collins either. I imagine he does not share that same pedestal as his dear friend Charles. <br /><br />I can say that this was an amazing blend of actual history and fantasy tossed together in a haze of opium smoke.And I think Wilkie is the best one to have based this tale upon. He was an interesting person. That is a fact. He led in interesting life. That too is a fact. He was also an opium addict. That too is a fact. I loved the telling of this story with his voice. I will forever be "Dear Reader" in my mind. <br /><br />I really am not sure of what to tell you, Dear Reader about this book. Only that I was taken back to London, walked those streets, suffered the stench of the filth and death. And I loved every minute of it. I spent time with Wilkie and Dickens in various places. I had a luncheon in a cemetery. I witnessed several of Dickens famous readings of his work. And I loved it all Dear Reader. I spent time in opium dens,in the bad parts of London. I loved that as well. <br /><br />Dear Reader, I wanted to call in sick to work so I could finish my grand adventure. But the REALITY is, I could not. If I was the kind of person to shirk my duties, I would have called in 3 days in a row and plowed through its 700 plus pages. But Dear Reader, I am not a shirker. And this was a most enjoyable journey through time. And I am glad it took a little extra time to read. <br /><br />Dear Reader , I want to offer just a bit of caution, women were so screwed. It pissed me off. I was able to count to 10 and move on. I suggest the same should you decide to attend this age. </span>
  <a data-text-id="600383836787490083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30079134" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9161592862349153662"><br/> Having been very fortunate to have an arc pass through my hands many months before publication, I want to say that Drood is a literary masterpiece that may enshrine Mr. Simmons as one of the top US writers of the present.<br/><br/> The last 5 years of Charles Dickens' life as told in a secret journal by younger disciple, friend and secret rival Wilkie Collins after the tragic train accident that turned Dickens life upside down.<br/><br/> Obsession, artistic creation, addiction and the dark recesses of the human mi</span>
  <span id="freeText9161592862349153662" style="display:none"><br /> Having been very fortunate to have an arc pass through my hands many months before publication, I want to say that Drood is a literary masterpiece that may enshrine Mr. Simmons as one of the top US writers of the present.<br /><br /> The last 5 years of Charles Dickens' life as told in a secret journal by younger disciple, friend and secret rival Wilkie Collins after the tragic train accident that turned Dickens life upside down.<br /><br /> Obsession, artistic creation, addiction and the dark recesses of the human mind and of London of the late 1860's are the main subjects of the book, while the two main protagonists Charles Dickens and the narrator Wilkie Collins will remain with the reader for a long time.<br /><br /> There are so many details and interesting stuff in this novel to satisfy any Dickens or Collins fan, any Victoriana fan, mysteries, magic or the belief in it, underground cults, the genesis of several famous novels, the beginning of authorial public performances, the writer as a celebrity...<br /><br /> Highly, highly recommended and a very engrossing though not a very easy read <br /><br /> I expect this book to compete for all literary awards of 09 for which it will be eligible</span>

<div id="review_75010146" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75010146" />
    <a title="Karla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2846441-karla"><img alt="Karla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537371209p2/2846441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75010146?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karla" href="/user/show/2846441-karla">Karla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75010146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4628046350402399277">The book really is misnamed and misleading. "Drood" is really a novelized biography of Dickens and Collins' relationship.<br/><br/>While I loved the attention to atmosphere and details of both Collins' and Dickens' lives, I was burning for the book to have a point & eventually felt as disappointed as I was with another long epic with a wimpy, pointless conclusion: The Stand by Stephen King. For most of the book, I had the sense that Simmons was trying to imitate the long-winded expository style of the nov</span>
  <span id="freeText4628046350402399277" style="display:none">The book really is misnamed and misleading. "Drood" is really a novelized biography of Dickens and Collins' relationship.<br /><br />While I loved the attention to atmosphere and details of both Collins' and Dickens' lives, I was burning for the book to have a point &amp; eventually felt as disappointed as I was with another long epic with a wimpy, pointless conclusion: The Stand by Stephen King. For most of the book, I had the sense that Simmons was trying to imitate the long-winded expository style of the novels from Collins' and Dickens' time. If so, he succeeded; however, it's not a light read and on more than one occasion it felt like he had information and darn it, he was going to include it even if it made scenes interminable.<br /><br />This reminded me of Simmons' book Carrion Comfort - huge, detailed, but endlessly circling and then meandering off into nowhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4628046350402399277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_43173341" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43173341" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/155411-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400181685p2/155411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43173341?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/155411-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43173341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11818998385792452585">I should start off by saying that I loved <i>The Terror</i>. I had no issues with the length of the novel; the story was completely compelling. If <i>Drood</i> had any of the dramatic tension present in Simmons' previous book, I would have finished it with aplomb; sadly, for me it had NO dramatic tension. My friend Jeff noted in his review that a good chunk of this book could have been excised at the editorial level and I now have to agree. I made it through 350 pages and what little Simmons would toss me in </span>
  <span id="freeText11818998385792452585" style="display:none">I should start off by saying that I loved <i>The Terror</i>. I had no issues with the length of the novel; the story was completely compelling. If <i>Drood</i> had any of the dramatic tension present in Simmons' previous book, I would have finished it with aplomb; sadly, for me it had NO dramatic tension. My friend Jeff noted in his review that a good chunk of this book could have been excised at the editorial level and I now have to agree. I made it through 350 pages and what little Simmons would toss me in the way of "bones" was insufficient to keep me interested.<br /><br />I do admire the amount of research that went into the book. Some part of me does want to know how it all turns out - just not enough to wage through 400+ more pages of very slow pacing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11818998385792452585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1967447329" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1967447329" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29647641-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520567409p2/29647641.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1967447329?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/29647641-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316120616" href="/book/show/8487283-drood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29647641-nathan?shelf=x2018-02-february">x2018-02-february</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1967447329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15347439131082217051">Sci-Fi, Horror, Historical Fiction, Thriller...my experience with Dan Simmons is always the same. He's a good writer and storyteller and I enjoy his books very much...even though he's sometimes (Ok, always) a bit more long-winded than he needs to be. If you're in the mood to wallow in a 500-1000 page world for a few days (or weeks, I'm a fast reader) Simmons is your guy. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('inUExqYD7nsgeqMx0CCf9lh+e+Cx/uOs3LJjpdSWt2spaQXPXQocObpU70WExXfE2b+7ra5zm9BlHCQbWvmdOA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BbjhH1kxCM+00CcCIT5m1e2mLcehn7h/8x8P1qpqbZKmpOAWojj6jS7+a3Z1247nbGftir4SwANKsUhoJAVHwQ==')})
  }

C’è un io e c’è un noi: anzi, un noantri. E c’è un bambino che impara a vivere dalle persone che gli sta<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4750306317287879727\" style=\"display:none\">La gioia fa parecchio rumore è un romanzo tempestoso che ubbidisce a una sola regola: dire la vita con tutta l’energia che ci si ritrova addosso. C’è un io e c’è un noi: anzi, un noantri. E c’è un bambino che impara a vivere dalle persone che gli stanno intorno: «gente che non si tiene niente nel cuore», allegra, chiassosa, abituata ad amare anche nei momenti più bui, e ad amare senza misura. Bonvissuto canta la Roma, ma canta soprattutto un amore assoluto, fulminante, che si accende nell’animo per trasformarlo.\n\nMescolando alto e basso a ogni riga, divagazioni e scene formidabili, il nuovo libro di Bonvissuto parte come un trattatello filosofico sull’amore per diventare a poco a poco un romanzo corale di grande forza. A differenza di molte passioni, quella calcistica dura una vita intera e arde sempre, nel bene e soprattutto nel male: «Forse il calcio è l’unica cosa al mondo che è più bella quando la fanno gli altri, quelli con quella maglia però. Che comunque ce l’hanno solo in prestito, perché la maglia della Roma è la mia. Potrebbero anche averla rubata. E l’amore forse è questo: correre appresso a un ladro che ci ha rubato qualcosa». Attorno a questa fiamma si condensa un microcosmo di padri, nonni, zii, fratelli di fede giallorossa, una comunità vera e propria, allegra, sterminata, capace d’iniziarti alla vita. La condivisione delle sconfitte, il divano da cui tutta la famiglia «guarda» la radio, l’epica costruzione della bandiera da portare allo stadio insieme ai panini con la frittata, le trasferte su quel pulmino lentissimo che profuma di mandarini, e le partite, certo, viste con occhi bambini ancora allergici a date, nomi, tecnicismi, ma capaci di vedere pure l’invisibile. Poi c’è Barabba, che vive in una roulotte lungo la ferrovia: spetterà a lui svelare al bambino la quantità di universi concentrati in una sola maglia di calcio. La numero cinque. La indossa un brasiliano atipico, un centrocampista che arriva in punta di piedi e realizza il sogno proibito di tutti i tifosi, l’innominabile parola che inizia con la s… La gioia fa parecchio rumore è uno di quei libri che ti fanno immergere totalmente nel mondo che raccontano. E che te lo fanno rimpiangere, alla fine, come se fosse il tuo.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4750306317287879727\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">About Dan Simmons</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1427999015p3/2687.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">Dan Simmons
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,054
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12443473466796421405"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br/><br/>Dan received his Master</span>
  <span id="freeText12443473466796421405" style="display:none"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br /><br />Dan received his Masters in Education from Washington University in St. Louis in 1971. He then worked in elementary education for 18 years—2 years in Missouri, 2 years in Buffalo, New York—one year as a specially trained BOCES "resource teacher" and another as a sixth-grade teacher—and 14 years in Colorado.<br /> <br /> ABOUT DAN<br /> Biographic Sketch <br /> <br />His last four years in teaching were spent creating, coordinating, and teaching in APEX, an extensive gifted/talented program serving 19 elementary schools and some 15,000 potential students. During his years of teaching, he won awards from the Colorado Education Association and was a finalist for the Colorado Teacher of the Year. He also worked as a national language-arts consultant, sharing his own "Writing Well" curriculum which he had created for his own classroom. Eleven and twelve-year-old students in Simmons' regular 6th-grade class averaged junior-year in high school writing ability according to annual standardized and holistic writing assessments. Whenever someone says "writing can't be taught," Dan begs to differ and has the track record to prove it. Since becoming a full-time writer, Dan likes to visit college writing classes, has taught in New Hampshire's Odyssey writing program for adults, and is considering hosting his own Windwalker Writers' Workshop.<br /><br />Dan's first published story appeared on Feb. 15, 1982, the day his daughter, Jane Kathryn, was born. He's always attributed that coincidence to "helping in keeping things in perspective when it comes to the relative importance of writing and life."<br /><br />Dan has been a full-time writer since 1987 and lives along the Front Range of Colorado—in the same town where he taught for 14 years—with his wife, Karen, his daughter, Jane, (when she's home from Hamilton College) and their Pembroke Welsh Corgi, Fergie. He does much of his writing at Windwalker—their mountain property and cabin at 8,400 feet of altitude at the base of the Continental Divide, just south of Rocky Mountain National Park. An 8-ft.-tall sculpture of the Shrike—a thorned and frightening character from the four Hyperion/Endymion novels—was sculpted by an ex-student and friend, Clee Richeson, and the sculpture now stands guard near the isolated cabin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12443473466796421405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3257056-drood">Quotes from Drood</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The beauty of that June day was almost staggering. After the wet spring, everything that could turn green had outdone itself in greenness and everything that could even dream of blooming or blossoming was in bloom and blossom. The sunlight was a benediction. The breezes were so caressingly soft and intimate on the skin as to be embarrassing.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/746877-the-beauty-of-that-june-day-was-almost-staggering-after">49 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;When the last autumn of Dickens's life was over, he continued to work through his final winter and into spring. This is how all of us writers give away the days and years and decades of our lives in exchange for stacks of paper with scratches and squiggles on them. And when Death calls, how many of us would trade all those pages, all that squandered lifetime-worth of painfully achieved scratches and squiggles, for just one more day, one more fully lived and experienced day? And what price would we writers pay for that one extra day spent with those we ignored while we were locked away scratching and squiggling in our arrogant years of solipsistic isolation?<br /><br />Would we trade all those pages for a single hour? Or all of our books for one real minute?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/745201-when-the-last-autumn-of-dickens-s-life-was-over-he">28 likes</a>
